About The Chinese Metaphysics Reference Series
The Chinese Metaphysics Reference Series of books are designed
primarily to be used as complementary textbooks for scholars, students,
researchers, teachers and practitioners of Chinese Metaphysics.
The goal is to provide quick easy reference tables, diagrams and charts,
facilitating the study and practice of various Chinese Metaphysics
subjects including Feng Shui, BaZi, Yi Jing, Zi Wei, Liu Ren, Ze Ri, Tai Yi, Qi Men and
Mian Xiang.
These series of books are intended as reference text and educational materials
principally for the academic syllabuses of the Mastery Academy of Chinese
Metaphysics. The contents have also been formatted so that Feng Shui practitioners
and other teachers of Chinese Metaphysics will always have a definitive source of
reference at hand, when teaching or applying their art.
Because each school of Chinese Metaphysics is different, the Reference Series of
books usually do not contain any specific commentaries, application methods or
explanations on the theory behind the formulas presented in its contents. This is
to ensure that the contents can be used freely and independently by all Feng Shui
Masters and teachers of Chinese Metaphysics without conflict.
If you would like to study or learn the applications of any of the formulas presented
in the Reference Series of books, we recommend that you undertake the courses
offered by Joey Yap and his team of Instructors at the Mastery Academy of Chinese
Metaphysics.

Preface
As a lifelong student, practitioner and champion of Chinese Metaphysics, I feel
the need to continuously improve both my own understanding and application
as well as the accessibility and profile of the subject itself. My dream is to bring
Chinese Metaphysics to the masses.
Many aspects of Chinese Metaphysics have been shrouded in secrecy
since ancient times, when knowledge was passed down from generation to
generation selectively. I abhor this practice, and when I began my journey in
this field two decades ago I vowed to put a stop to it.
I know how this situation began. In times gone by, our ancestors recognized
the danger of allowing Chinese Metaphysical wisdom to fall into the wrong
hands. One way of preventing this was to only share it with those who were
considered 'worthy'. The problem with this idea is that people who are not seen
or appreciated as being 'worthy' are overlooked. Genuine, talented people
have long been denied access to information they could do wonders with.
What's more, incomplete information can do more harm than the truth. There
are more problems. This closed, exclusive learning structure encourages blind
faith, a lack of inter-school comparison and destroys any chance for students
to improve and refine the subject over time.
Over the course of my career I've broken the cycle somewhat by making
Feng Shui and BaZi available to everyone. Enthusiasts all over the world can
now study and master these once exclusive subjects using my books and
reference materials. I'm very proud to have played my part in this process and
to have written materials that have encouraged other modern day masters to
open up and share their knowledge. The application and spread of Chinese
Metaphysics enhances life satisfaction and control, and benefits everyone.
The mission isn't over. For the past year, I've had my eye on one exception
to the above. One stubborn nut I haven't managed to crack yet. It's called Qi
Men Dun Jia.
Qi Men Dun Jia – or Qi Men as it is often abbreviated – is older than BaZi,
Feng Shui or ZeRi. In fact, it is believed to be one of the founding Chinese
Metaphysical sciences. Despite being so well established, Qi Men doesn't
really get the recognition it deserves. Over the centuries, its top secrets have
remained guarded. Even as recently as a decade ago, there wasn't much
published literature available for the common reader, even in the Chinese
speaking world. It is this lack of accessibility combined with the cryptic
complexity of Qi Men that has prevented it from obtaining the respect and
appreciation it rightfully deserves. Until recently, only those studying under
the direct tutelage of a Qi Men master have been able to benefit from this
ancient art.
Fortunately, things have been improving. Chinese masters and authors
have begun to see the benefits of transparency and they've been working to
share some of their exclusive knowledge. Slowly, people have begun to take
notice.

My study of Qi Men Dun Jia has put me in contact with many masters in this
field. Over the past two decades I have exchanged much of my own experience
in Feng Shui, BaZi and Yi Jing in order to gain access to their trade secrets.
It has not been easy to gain permission to share what I have learned with my
own students.
Qi Men is an undeniably difficult subject - difficult, but worthy of attention. It is
highly versatile and can be employed in many different scenarios and situations.
The great challenge has been to somehow compile everything known about Qi
Men into one definitive book. I made it my mission to tackle this herculean task,
and I believe that my efforts have paid off. There has never been a book like the
one you're holding right now - much less in English!
Originally, I had planned to develop a Home Study course on Qi Men. My
idea was that every chapter would contain research materials and application
examples, so that eventually students would come to have a complete
background understanding of the system as well as the ability to apply what
they had learned. I realized quickly that covering all the bases in this way
would necessitate at least 3,000 pages of supporting material. Qi Men is a rich
subject indeed. After much toil, it became clear to me that some kind of division
between reference materials and practical application techniques would be
required. I decided to make this distinction by reserving practical application
techniques for specific titles - Qi Men Feng Shui, Qi Men Date and Activity
Selection, Qi Men Destiny Analysis, and others. This means that students and
practitioners can apply Qi Men with ease, and then dig deeper into reference
materials if they need to, rather than sifting through reference materials to get
to the practical stuff when they need it.
Sorting everything in this way was no easy task. I had to dissect the ancient
texts and manuscripts, and carefully rearrange and explain everything in a
new, compartmentalized way. It doesn't help that Qi Men tackles very broad
concerns. BaZi, Feng Shui and Yi Jing all deal with very specific questions
and pursuits, Qi Men Dun Jia deals with much broader possibilities. It is
a multifaceted system that is connected to other branches of Chinese
Metaphysics studies, and this enables it to perform four integral functions
instead of one - namely, Forecasting, Date and Activity Selection, Feng Shui
and Destiny Analysis.
Another challenge in writing this book was the fact that there are at least four
competing schools of Qi Men Dun Jia thought: the San Yuan Qi Men 三元奇門,
the Celestial Qi Men 禽遁, the Fa Qi Men 法奇門, and the Flying Palace Small
Qi Men 飛宮小奇門. What is so irksome about them is that they honor their own
interpretation of the Chart plotting process (Rotating Palace 轉宮法 or Flying
Palace 飛宮法) and their own assessment of the Qi Men Calendar (The Qi

With an appreciation of the daunting task ahead, I embarked on a one-year
mission to acquire additional resources – both modern and ancient – in a road
trip in Taiwan and Hong Kong. There, I sniffed out ancient reference texts and
met with Qi Men masters. I combined what I found with my own library of
information and then, through a long, difficult process, rearranged everything
into ONE book: the Qi Men Compendium you are holding now.
What you get in this book is a collection of information from ALL Qi Men
Schools and direct access to information that has taken me years to compile,
even with access to ancient classics and guidance from great masters of the
art. I want this book to not only be utilized by my own students, but also by
masters, practitioners and genuine enthusiasts who practice other schools of
Qi Men Dun Jia. I've taken every effort to represent the ancient texts in simple
modern English without giving my personal opinion on whether the information
is in line with the school of thought I personally advocate. This means that the
book is suitable for everyone as it presents the facts without prejudice.
It is my vision to make this book the definitive reference book for Qi Men Dun
Jia. Although I wish I had such a book when I started my study on Qi Men so
many years ago, I'm happy that new students may now enjoy full access to
the topic.
Finally, for the first time in history, everyone can learn and benefit from Qi Men
Dun Jia.

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia
Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 3
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
A
What is Qi Men Dun Jia?
It was recorded that Oracle Methods had been used in ancient China
since the time of the Warring States Period in 700 BC some 3,000 years
ago. Although according to some sources, it dates back further to the
Yellow Emperor period, 5,000 years ago. These methods are collectively
known as the Three Sacred Methods or Three Styles (San Shi 三式),
China’s premier metaphysical art, which are used mainly to forecast or
calculate the outcomes or luck in relation to the aspects of time and
space (direction). The Three Sacred Methods are Tai Yi Shen Shu 太乙
神數, Liu Ren Shen Ke 六壬神課 and Qi Men Dun Jia 奇門遁甲.
The transliterated name of Qi Men Dun Jia 奇門遁甲 - Mystical Doors
Escaping Technique - lends an air of mystique and magic at first mention,
often giving people the wrong impression on what this ancient study
truly is. “Mysterious” it may be to an extent; “magical” or “enchantment”
it is definitely not.
By deconstructing its name, “Qi” 奇 points to the mysterious, rare and
unusual (as opposed to the energy Qi); “Men” 門 literally means “door”
or “gate”; “Dun” 遁 is “hide” or “escape to remain hidden”; “Jia” 甲 is
the first of the ten Heavenly Stems, or a coded military reference to the
General or leader. Combine and transliterate these words, it becomes
“Mysterious Doors Hiding the Jia”, which sounds quite puzzling.
Therefore, it is more concise to translate Qi Men Dun Jia as Mystical
Doors Escaping Technique.
What makes Qi Men Dun Jia more applicable to the modern world
compared to the other two Oracle Methods? The Tai Yi Shen Shu
method predicts major large-scale events within countries such as
natural disasters like tsunamis and earthquakes, to human catastrophes
like massacres and wars. Liu Ren Shen Ke is a method used to divine
outcomes of daily personal events that require important big decisions.
Both methods are potent in their own right, but in today’s context, they
have become irrelevant due to modern technology and how lifestyle has
evolved. Which leave us with Qi Men Dun Jia – the only method highly
relevant in the modern context.

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia

A
How Qi Men Dun Jia Works
Placing people “in the right place at the right time” is Qi Men Dun Jia’s
domain. It utilises a technique for calculating time and space that is
present for the past 3,000 years. Thus, it can isolate with great accuracy
the exact moments in time to which one can embark on a specific action
to produce the desired outcome. In the military strategy context, Qi Men
is mainly used to identify the right time to attack the enemy, the right
place to do it, and when to carry out the attack to assure success in
the plan.
As mentioned earlier, Qi Men doesn’t conjure magic or miracles -
although it is easy for many to misconstrue it as something of the
occult, due to its effective and accurate results. False claims that attest
its power that can allow one to escape fate and destiny, or even cheat
death only makes people more fearful about Qi Men for the very wrong
reasons!
Qi Men actually enhances the absorption of beneficial Qi energy to
boost one’s potential for success. To sum it up, this technique has the
ability to help you improve your chance of attaining specific objectives.
There are four major schools to Qi Men:
• The Astronomical Qi Men 禽遁, which was passed on through
lineage and did not survive the course of time
• The San Yuan Method 三元奇門 that is based on the 64
Hexagrams of Xuan Kong Da Gua
• Fa Qi Men 法奇門, a method associated with Daoism, which
touches upon supernatural forces and talisman
• Fei Gong Xiao Qi Men 飛宮小奇門 (Flying Palace Small Qi
Men) or the abridged version of Qi Men

-- 16 of 810 --

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia
Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 5
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
A
Qi Men shares a common base as all other aspects of Chinese
Metaphysics. It is rooted in the Heavenly Stems and Earthly Branches,
and has the He Tu, Luo Shu, the Yin and Yang, the Five Elements, 9
Palaces, the 9 Stars, the Eight Directions and the Constellations as
its basic principles as well. It combines both the energy computation
normally associated with Feng Shui and the astronomical calculations
related to BaZi or Zi Wei. It is essentially a system that breaks down the
essence of time, computing the energy that existed in the environment
during each year, month, day and hour using a Qi Men chart.

-- 17 of 810 --

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia
6 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
A
History of Qi Men Dun Jia
The history of Qi Men Dun Jia began as a legend, where it was believed
that the Goddess of the Nine Heaven 九天玄女 has bestowed this art to
the Yellow Emperor 黃帝. He then used it as a military strategy to win
the war against the rebel called Chi You 蚩尤.
Qi Men Dun Jia was also applied by legendary Chinese military
strategists like Zhuge Liang 諸葛亮, Jian Zi Ya 姜子牙, Zhang Liang
張良, 	Liu Bo Wen 劉伯溫 and many others as a useful skill to form
military strategies and tactics:
Zhuge Liang 諸葛亮
Zhuge Liang 諸葛亮 (181 BC – 234 BC) was the greatest and most
accomplished strategists during the Three Kingdoms Era of China,
noted for deploying the techniques of Qi Men to win battles. In the
events leading to the Battle of the Red Cliffs and during Red Cliffs
itself, Zhuge Liang roped in the art of Qi Men where it emerged as
the winning strategy for both battles.

-- 18 of 810 --

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia
Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 7
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
A
Zhuge Liang’s Technique: Borrowing the East Wind
The Battle of Red Cliffs was fought between the allied forces of
the Southern warlords, Liu Bei 劉備 and Sun Quan 孫權, and
the Northern warlords, Cao Cao 曹操. Having dabbled in Qi Men
during an episode prior to this battle in Red Cliffs, Zhuge Liang 諸
葛亮 relied on the technique again when he wanted to weaken the
navy power of Cao Cao by setting fire to the Wei fleet of ships.
He realized that for this fire attack to succeed, the wind must blow
from the East or else his own fleet of ships would be ablaze. Zhuge
Liang used Qi Men to predict the timing of the East wind and the
right moment to carry out the attack. To buy time and to surround
the entire strategy with a sense of mystery, a Seven Stars Altar was
set up, in which Zhuge Liang performed elaborate rituals to adjure the
“help” from the East wind.
As predicted, the East wind blew in full force just before the fire
attack was carried out. The full-on fire attack dented Wei’s navy
and motivation - thanks to a combination of Zhuge Liang’s strong
execution and the Qi Men techniques.
Jiang Ziya 姜子牙
He was a legendary figure and the famous Chinese military strategist
who helped King Wen and King Wu of Zhou overthrow the Shang
Dynasty (1600 BC - 1046 BC). He was an expert in military affairs
and noted for using Qi Men in his strategies and planning. Under his
direction, Zhou managed to overthrow the tyrannical Shang and as
a result, Jiang Ziya remained as the most notable Prime Minister and
“the master of strategy” – both of which helped the Zhou government
to flourish and grow far stronger than that of Shang Dynasty.

-- 19 of 810 --

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia
8 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
A
Zhang Liang 張良
It is said that Zhang Liang (262 BC – 189 BC) learnt Qi Men from
Huang Shi Gong – one of the Four Haos of Mount Shang, a group
of four reclusive wise men - and used it to help Emperor Gao Zu to
conquer the whole of China and establish the Han Dynasty. Zhang
Liang is also known as one of the “Three Heroes of the early Han
Dynasty”, along with Han Xin and Xiao He.
Liu Bowen 劉伯溫
Liu Ji (1311 - 1375) or better known as Liu Bowen, was the key
advisor to Zhu Yuanzhang, the emperor and founder of the Ming
Dynasty. The version of the Qi Men system we that use today was
believed to be deployed by Liu Bowen to help Ming Dynasty rise to
the throne.
Mao Zedong 毛澤東
It was believed that Mao Zedong (1893 – 1976), Chairman of the
Communist Party of China, actually tapped into the techniques of
Qi Men as he dictated specific timing and direction for the troop
to be deployed, a signature of Qi Men. As a Chinese revolutionary,
political theorist and communist leader, Mao led the People’s
Republic of China (PRC) from its establishment in 1949 until his
death in 1976.
In ancient China, Qi Men can only be practiced by the Emperor and
selected advisors. It was so closely guarded that anyone else caught
practicing it will face execution. This restricts its teachings and
dissemination to an elite few, which further add to the enigma of the
subject.
Today, Qi Men is fully employed by many, as it can be applied to
competitive environments in deriving the best strategic decision for
various scenarios. It now helps people in important decision-making for
real-life situations related to business, career, relationship, health and
so on. Applying Qi Men can increase the chances of success and also
reveal talents.

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia
Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 9
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
A
Understanding the Meaning of Qi Men Dun Jia
As the name suggests, Qi Men is often considered as the Chinese
cosmic game of chess. In chess, the objective is always to protect and
advance the King. In Qi Men, depending on your goal, the idea is to
either hide the Jia 甲 or find it in the Qi Men chart.
1. Qi 奇:
This refers to the Three Wonders 三奇 - Yi 乙, Bing 丙 and Ding 丁
– which are also the Three Heavenly Stems. Yi 乙 is known as the
Sun Noble 日奇, Bing 丙 is the Moon Noble 月奇, and Ding 丁 is the
Star Noble 星奇.
2. Men 門:
This refers to the 8 Doors 八門, which consists of Open 開, Rest 休,
Life 生, Harm 傷, Hide 杜, Fear 驚, Scene 景 and Death 死.
• Open 開門 represents breaking out and kicking off the obstacle.
• Rest 休門 represents being calm, contented.
• Life 生門 represents the abundance, growth and prosperity.
• Harm 傷門 represents the lack of resources, harm and pain.
• Hide 杜門 represents a blockage, darkness and secrets.
• Fear 惊門 represents the anxiety, nervousness and impatience.
• Scenery 景門 represents beauty and appearances.
• Death 死門 represents darkness or Yin Qi 陰气 and the failure to
survive.
Open, Rest and Life Doors are indicated as auspicious.
Scene Door is denoted as secondary auspicious.
Harm, Hide, Fear and Death Doors are indicated as inauspicious.

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia
10 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
A
3. Dun 遁:
Means to conceal or hide in the Six Yi 六儀.
4. Jia 甲:
• 	Jia 甲 is the leader of the Heavenly Stems. It plays a very important
role and dominates the Heavenly Stems. But, Geng Metal 庚金 is
the big threat for Jia because of the Five Elements cycle, where
Metal counters Wood. Meaning: Metal will harm and hurt the Jia
Wood.
• 	To protect Jia Wood, it needs the other Stems to help. First, it
needs the Yi Wood to combine with Geng Metal. Secondly Wood
can produce Fire, and Fire is the children for Wood. Therefore, Jia
Wood can manipulate the Yang Fire (Bing 丙) and Yin Fire (Ding 丁)
to counter Geng Metal, and negate the threat.
• 	Therefore, the Three Wonders 三奇 – Yi 乙, Bing 丙 and Ding 丁 -
are the Heavenly Stems that protect the Jia 甲. Jia is hidden in the
Six Yi.
The definition of Qi Men Dun Jia is certainly more than what was
explained in brief here. These will be elaborated as the subject is
explored further in the following chapters.

A. Introduction to Qi Men Dun Jia
Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 11
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
A
Qi Men Dun Jia & Its Usage
Qi Men Dun Jia can be used in four ways:
1. Forecasting
Qi Men uses different predictive methods to forecast different
scenarios or activities. It is also a technique that is able to review a
person’s past history to forecast the future. The past history can help
an individual adjust his/her behavior in the future in order to achieve
a specific outcome.
2. Date and Activity Selection
Unlike Date Selection or Ze Ri 擇日, Qi Men emphasizes more on the
time and location as opposed to the date. It has the ability to arrive at
the ‘golden moment’—that is, a specific hour in a day combined with
a specific direction and a specific action—to guarantee a successful
undertaking.
3. Feng Shui
Qi Men is able to calculate the luck analysis as well as the particular
direction for a chosen endeavour, such as starting a new business,
undertaking an important health procedure, travel, or embarking on
a new relationship or marriage.
4. Astrology
Astrology or Destiny Analysis uses a person’s birth date and time to
plot the Qi Men chart. The Day Heavenly Stem refers to the person’s
personality, inner thinking and motivation; the Month Heavenly Stem
indicates the person’s siblings; the Year Heavenly Stems represents
the person’s parents, while the Hour Heavenly Stems denotes
the person’s children. Therefore, relationship analysis between a
person and his/her parents, siblings and family can be interpreted.
In addition, through a reading of the Stem interactions, a person’s
career, study capability, wealth, health, relationship—i.e. destiny
outlook—can be read and analysed from the Qi Men chart. Unlike a
typical BaZi reading that is deconstructed from the natal chart and
readings of the Luck Pillar and Annual Pillar, Qi Men Destiny Analysis
is based on the individual year.

C.1. 	How to plot the Earth Plate地盤
Two things you need to know before you can
PLOT the Earth Plate 地盤.
1: The 3 Noble 6 Crescents 三奇六儀
The Earth Plate of a Qi Men Dun Jia Chart consists of TWO layers of
Heavenly Stems. These Heavenly Stems are known as the 3 Noble 6
Crescents 三奇六儀 (San Qi Liu Yi). There are a total of 9 Stems used
in Qi Men Dun Jia – 乙丙丁戊己庚辛壬癸.
The first step involves arranging the 3 Noble 6 Cresecents 三奇六儀
according to the sequence below:
1. 戊 Wu
2. 己 Ji
3. 庚 Geng
4. 辛 Xin
5. 壬 Ren
6. 癸 Gui
7. 丁 Ding
8. 丙 Bing
9. 乙 Yi

The next part requires you to plot the 3 Noble 6 Crescents 三奇六儀

Flow).

How do we know if we should use a YANG FLOW or a YIN FLOW?
This is determined by referring to the Qi Men Calendar. If the Date falls
under the YANG Dun 陽遁 (Yang Flow) dates, then it's a Yang Flow
chart. This usually means any date between Dec 21 and June 20 of
each year.
If the date falls under the YIN DUN 陰遁 (Yin Flow) dates, it's a Yin Flow
Chart. This usually means any date between June 21 and Dec 20.

C. How to plot a Qi Men Dun Jia Chart
38 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇
門

甲
寶
鑑
C
C.1. 	How to plot the Earth Plate地盤
Take note that the Luo Shu Numbers relate to the "starting" point of the
戊 Wu Stem. This means, if it is a 1 Structure, we should start with 戊 Wu
Stem in the North. If it is a 2 Structure, we start with 戊 Wu Stem in the
Southwest. If it is a 3 Structure, we start with 戊 Wu Stem in the East.
There are two types of structures: Yin Structure and
Yang Structures.
If it is a Yin Structure, the stream of stems beginning with 戊 Wu Stem
will be allocated in the REVERSE path of the Luo Shu.
If it is a Yang Structure, the stream of stems beginning with 戊 Wu Stem
will be allocated in the FORWARD path of the Luo Shu.
Plotting the Earth Plate Stems
a) Determine the Structure Yin and Yang, and Structure 局 Number.
b) Place Wu 戊 at the original sector of the Structure Number in the Luo
Shu Chart.

C.2 	How to plot the Heaven Plate 天盤
Step 1:
After plotting the Earth Plate Stems, we will now proceed to plot the Heaven
Plate Stems. To do this, you must first find the Lead Stem 符首 of the Earth
Plate 地盤. This is done by referencing the Hourly Stem's location in the Luo
Shu.
Refer to the Six Jia Streams Leader Table below to find the Lead Stem 符首
of the Earth Plate 地盤 based on the Hour Pillar. The location of that Stem is
the beginning point of the Heaven Plate 天盤.

C.2 	How to plot the Heaven Plate 天盤
Step 2: The Circular Path
There are TWO types of movements in the Nine Square Qi Men Chart.
a) 	The Luo Shu Path
b) 	The Circular Path
To plot the Heaven Plate, we need to follow the "Circular Path"
method.
From Step 1, after you've determined the location of the Lead Stem 符
首, arrange the stems of the Earth Plate, in a circular path around the
chart to form the Heaven Plate.
Please note that Heaven Plate is plotted following the Circular Cycle
in Qi Men Dun Jia. This is different with the plotting of the Earth Plate
Stems which is plotted following the flow of the Luo Shu path.
* Circular (Clockwise) Cycle arrangement:
If the Starting Point is from Qian, then it will continue on as follows:
Qian 乾 (NW)  Kan 坎 (N)  Gen 艮 (NE)  Zhen 震 (E)  Xun 巽 (SE) 
Li 離 (S)  Kun 坤 (SW)  Dui 兌 (W)
巽 SE Xun 	離 S Li 	坤 SW Kun

C.2 	How to plot the Heaven Plate 天盤
Note: 	The Heaven Plate 天盤, 8 Doors 八門 and 9 Stars 九星 of
a Qi Men Dun Jia chart is plotted following the Circular
Cycle in a clockwise flow regardless of Yang Structure or
Yin Structure.
Only the 8 Deities 八神 are plotted following the Circular
Clockwise Cycle if is Yang Structure, and Anti-Clockwise
Cycle, if it is Yin Structure.

C
C.3 	How to plot the 8 Doors 八門
The Eight Doors are allocated on the Qi Men Chart using the CIRCULAR
clockwise method.
The task here is merely to determine WHICH of the 8 Palaces in the Qi
Men Chart will begin the 8 Door allocation.
1. 	In order to plot the 8 Doors, we first need to determine WHICH
is the Lead Door or better known in Qi Men Terms – the Envoy
Location 直使門 - and where this door is located in our Qi
Men Chart.
2. 	Then, from the location of the Earth Plate Lead Stem 地盤符首,
calculate using the Hour Pillar’s Stream Leader, using the Luo Shu
Path all the way UNTIL you reach the Hour Pillar. This Palace is the
START position of the 8 Doors.
3. 	Locate the Envoy in the start position and plot in a CIRCULAR
method the rest of the 8 Doors.
Here are a few things we need to bear in mind:
We need to observe Yin and Yang Dun Qi. So it is important to take
note if the chart belongs to the Yin or Yang flow. If the date of the
chart belongs to Yang Dun, this means we need to use the FORWARD
Luo Shu Path. If it is Yin Dun (Yin Flow) chart, this means we need to
use the Reverse Luo Shu Path.

Remember - the location of the Earth Plate Lead Stem 地盤符首 is known
as the Envoy Door 直使門 or the Lead Door. Usually in Qi Men literature,
the term sEnvoy 直使 and Envoy Door 直使門 mean the same thing.
For instance, if Earth Plate Lead Stem 地盤符首 is at the Xun Palace 巽
宮, then the Envoy 直使門 for this chart will be the door of Delusion 杜
(Refer to the Basic Natal Chart of the 8 Doors). This means Envoy =
Door of Delusion.

C.4 	How to plot the 9 Stars 九星
The location of the Lead Stem 符首 in is the starting point of the 9 Stars.
The Lead Stem is found based on the HOUR PILLAR. You can refer to
the Six Jia Stream Leader Table 六甲旬符首表 to find the Lead Stem
符首.
Step 1 :
First, we need to determine the location of the Lead Stem 符首. Take
note of which Palace is the location of this Lead Stem 符首 in the Qi
Men Chart. Then, refer to the original Natal 9 Stars Chart to find out
which is the natal star of this Palace.

Structure, the 9 Stars are plotted according to the Clockwise Cycle.
The sequence of the 9 Stars according to the Clockwise Cycle is as
follows:
Grass 天蓬  Ambassador 天任  Destructor 天沖  Assistant 天輔 
Hero 天英  Grain 天芮(Bird 天禽)  Pillar 天柱  Heart 天心

C
C.5 	How to plot the 8 Deities 八神
There are a total of 8 Deities in Qi Men Dun Jia. These are Chief 符,
Snake 蛇, Moon 陰, Harmony 合, Hook 陳, Phoenix 雀, Earth 地,
Heaven 天. The leader of these 8 Deities is called – The Lead Star (Zhi
Fu 直符).
You will notice that the term Lead Star 值符 is previously used to describe
the 'Leading' Star of the 9 Stars 九星 stream. It is in fact referring to the
SAME thing. Thus plotting the 8 Deities is merely a simple continuation
from the 9 Stars.
Step 1:
First locate the Chief 直符. This is extremely easy to find. The location of
the Hourly Stem is found in the location of the Lead Star.
Step 2:
After you have located the Chief 直符, arrange the rest of the 8 Deities
following the Clockwise Cycle. Please take note that the ORDER of
the 8 Deities arrangement will go either the Forward flow, if it is Yang
Structure, or the Reverse flow, if it is Yin Structure.

Step 5: Plot the 9 Stars 九星
a) Lead Stem Ji 己 is located at the Xun 巽 (Southeast) Palace. Where
the Natal 9 Star Chart is the Heavenly Assistant 天輔. Thus the Lead
Star of this chart is Assistant 輔.
b) Place Lead Star Assistant 輔 into the Palace where the Hourly Stem
is located. In this case because it is the Wu Yin 戊寅 Hour, Wu 戊
is located in the Center Palace and already positioned at the Kun
(Southwest) Palace. Place the Lead Star Assistant 輔 in the Kun
(Southwest) Palace.
c) From Kun (Southwest) Palace, arrange the sequence of 9 Stars
according to the Clockwise Cycle into the chart.
Assistant 天輔  Hero 天英  Grain 天芮(Bird 天禽)  Pillar 天柱 
Heart 天心  Grass 天蓬  Ambassador 天任  Destructor 天沖

The Ten Heavenly Stems in Qi Men Dun Jia
According to an age-old Chinese legend, there used to be “Ten Suns” up
in the sky, causing extreme heat and unbearable weather conditions on
Earth. One day, the legendary Hou Yi 后羿 shot down nine of these Suns
using his magical bow and arrows, leaving only one. He saved the earth
from overheating and allowed perfect condition for life to flourish. What
remains in the sky, is the Sun we have in our Solar System today.
Some scholars of metaphysics world believe that the foundation and
existence of the Heavenly Stems can be related back to the magnetic
field effect that is generated from the interactions among the planets in
the Solar System.
In essence, based on The Ten Chinese Suns myth, “Jia 甲” would be the
first among all the “Suns” and it can be regarded as the Solar effect.
To expand on this idea, if Jia is the Sun, Yi 乙 would be Mercury, Bing 丙
is Venus, Ding 丁 is Saturn, Wu 戊 is Jupiter, Geng 庚 is Mars, Ren 壬 is
Earth, Ji 己 is Uranus, Xin 辛 is Neptune, and Gui 癸is Pluto.
The ten symbols of the Heavenly Stems that we have today are perhaps
the result of long-term observation of our astronomical phenomena.
The original image of the Heavenly Stems and Earthly Branches is
derived from the use of a “tree” as an analogy. Ancient Chinese sages
explained that the “Heavenly Stem is like the stem of a tree, strong and
hence, Yang in nature; Earthly Branch is like the branch of a tree, subtle
and thus, Yin in nature.” Based on the analogy of a “tree”, there is an
interesting illustration of each Stem and the Chinese character chosen
to represent the Stem:

-- 149 of 810 --

138 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
D
甲Jia
Yang Wood
A seed breaking out of the soil, contains Yin polarity inside
and also surrounded by Yin energies which governs growth
and life.
乙Yi
Yin Wood
A young tree growing, with soft leaves and branches bending
in the wind.
丙Bing
Yang Fire
Reflects the character “Bing 炳”, which means brightness, and
denotes everything that is bright and brilliant.
丁Ding
Yin Fire
Growing steadily and fast, just like a human being.
戊Wu
Yang Earth
The lower half of the character “Wu 茂”, which means
exuberance.
己Ji
Yin Earth
Emulates the characters of “Qi 起” and “Ji 紀” and indicates
that everything thrives after a period of restraint, and that
things will take shape soon after.
庚Geng
Yang Metal
Echos the enunciation of “Geng 更”, and represents the
waiting for the next spring season after the harvest period in
Autumn.
辛Xin
Yin Metal
Resonates the meaning of “Xin 辛” (Spice) with indications
that everything will turn out “tasteful” after matters begin to
take shape. As Xin also has similar pronunciation as “Xin”
(New), it means that everything will change and renew.
壬Ren
Yang Water
Reflects the character “Ren 妊” (Pregnant). It shows that
Yang lurks underneath the soil and everything is still being
conceived.
癸Gui
Yin Water
Derived from the character “Gui 揆”, which highlights that
everything is being concealed and contained. Whatever it is
that is developing under the soil, it is waiting to break out.

The Ten Heavenly Stems - Jia 甲, Yi 乙, Bing 丙, Ding 丁, Wu 戊, Ji 己,
Geng 庚, Xin 辛, Ren 壬, Gui 癸 - represent Yang polarity. From a spatial
perspective, they are in correspondence with the directions in the Qi
Men Nine Palaces (9 Sector Grid).
The Later Heaven Chart (Luo Shu Nine Palaces) represents the energy
of the Earth while Heavenly Stems represent the energy of Heaven or
space. The nine Palaces used in Qi Men Dun Jia allow us to map out the
Heavenly Stems, (Heavenly energy), and assess their effect on Earth,
generating different changes, respond and reactions.
The Heavenly Stems have a “natal” position in the Luo Shu Palace and
they are paired up in different location: Jia 甲 and Yi 乙 in the East, Bing
丙 and Ding 丁 in the South, Geng 庚 and Xin 辛 in the West, Ren 壬 and
Gui 癸 in the North, and Wu 戊 and Ji 己 in the Center.

The Ten Heavenly Stems are considered the Yang Factor, while the Luo
Shu represents the Yin Factor of our cosmos.
The Stems are therefore only confined to palaces that have an ODD
number of Luo Shu. This is because ODD belongs to Yang polarity. Jia
甲 and Yi 乙 in the East correspond with the Zhen 3 House; Bing 丙 and
Ding 丁 in the South correspond with the Li 9 House; Geng 庚 and Xin 辛
in the West correspond with the Dui 7 House; Ren 壬 and Gui 癸 in the
North correspond with the Kan 1 House; Wu 戊 and Ji 己 in the Center
correspond with the Center 5 House. In a nutshell, all the Stems would
be in the Luo Shu palaces if their numbers are Yang (odd) numbers such
as 1, 3, 5, 7 or 9.

However, Yang polarity is dynamic in nature. As a symbol of time, the
combinations between the Ten Heavenly Stems and the Twelve Earthly
Branches are dynamic and constantly moving. This represents the orbit
of our solar system as well as the natural movement of Qi. The Stems
and Branches will overlap and move in a clockwise direction, with the
Yang Stems matching the Yang Branches.

By now the Ten Heavenly Stems have combined with the Twelve Earthly
Branches for six times, forming a total of sixty Stem-Branch pairings.
This is also known as the “Sixty Hua Jia Zi” 六十花甲子. The 60 Jia Zi
represents the 60 Pillar cycle. Ancient Chinese used these 60 Jia Zi
cycles to keep a record of the year, month, day, and hour. The Qi Men
Dun Jia (Qi Men) system fits the 60 Jia Zi into the space and time model
of the Nine Palaces to simulate the movement of the planets and map
out their influences on Earth through the passage of time.
The ancient Chinese sages also gave the Ten Heavenly Stems the
attributes of Yin and Yang and have then assigned them an element
based on the Five Elements.
Yang Stems 	Odd Numbers

The Yang Stems: Jia 甲, Bing 丙, Wu 戊, Geng 庚, Ren 壬 are represented
by the odd numbers 1, 3, 5, 7, 9 respectively; while the Yin Stems of Yi
乙, Ding 丁, Ji 己, Xin 辛, Gui 癸 are represented by the even numbers
2, 4, 6, 8, 10.
The Ten Heavenly Stems are also given the attributes of the Five
Elements:
Jia 甲 and Yi 乙 in the East belong to Wood, with Jia 甲 as Yang Wood
and Yi 乙 as Yin Wood; Bing 丙 and Ding 丁 in the South belong to Fire,
Bing 丙 as Yang Fire and Ding 丁 as Yin Fire; Geng 庚 and Xin 辛 in the
West represent Metal, Geng 庚 as Yang Metal and Xin 辛 as Yin Metal;
Ren 壬 and Gui 癸 in the North represent Water, Ren 壬 as Yang Water
and Gui 癸 as Yin Water; Wu 戊 and Ji 己 in the center represent Earth,
Wu 戊 as Yang Earth and Ji 己 and Yin Earth.

Now that they are assigned with the attributes of the Five Elements, the
Ten Heavenly Stems, will have interactions like:
Reinforce
生
(Growth)
Restraint
克
(Control)
Harmony
合
(Combine)
Opposition
衝
(Clash)
The term “Reinforce” can also be translated as Growth or Produce. Its
interaction can be exemplified when Water reinforces (or produces or
strengthens) Wood, Wood reinforces Fire, Fire reinforces Earth, Earth
reinforces Metal, and Metal reinforces Water.
In the interaction of “Restraint” or Control, we can see that Water
restrains Fire, Fire restrains Metal, Metal restrains Wood, Wood restrains
Earth, and Earth restrains Water.
A “Harmony” or combination relationship is found when the stems
elements are maintained between their Yin and Yang balance. An
“Opposition” or clash happens in Yang to Yang, and Yin to Yin
interactions.
In detail:
1) The Harmony between Jia 甲 Yang Wood and Ji 己 Yin Earth, is called
Harmony of Righteousness (中正之合).
2) The Harmony between Yi 乙 Yin Wood and Geng 庚 Yang Metal, is
called Harmony of Benevolence (仁義之合).
3) The Harmony between Bing 丙 Yang Fire and Xin 辛 Yin Metal, is
called Harmony of Authority (權威之合).
4) The Harmony between Ding 丁 Yin Fire and Ren 壬 Yang Water, is
called Harmony of Lust (淫蕩之合).
5) The Harmony between Wu 戊 Yang Earth and Gui 癸 Wu Yin Water,
is called Harmony of Inaffection (無情之合).

In Metaphysical terms, an Opposition is synonymous with “Clash”, and
this means that the elements are opposing one another. The Clash
interaction is seen between the pairing of Jia 甲 and Geng 庚, Yi 乙 and
Xin 辛, Bing 丙 and Ren 壬, Ding 丁 and Gui 癸. This means that apart
from Wu 戊 and Ji 己, the Earth elements in the Center, the interaction
between the rest of Five Elements is Control, and when it comes to Yang
to Yang and Yin to Yin interaction, it is Clash.
This is illustrated in the chart below.

In the Qi Men system, different combos of Heavenly Stems are given
specific terms and various auspicious-inauspicious connotations. For
instance, Green Dragon Combines Spirit 青龍合靈, Young Maiden
Commits Adultery 幼女姦淫, or Dry Wood Meets Fierce Fire 乾柴烈火.

158 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇

甲

鑑
D
Such combos are “created” in the Qi Men Chart from the combination
between the Heaven and Earth Plate Stems. Some Structures are
auspicious, such as Green Dragon Returns 青龍返首, Flying Bird Fall
into Cave 飛鳥跌穴, while some are inherently inauspicious. All of these
combinations are applied to analyze or to read the Qi Map of a given
sector or time frame. These stem combos are read in conjunction with
the Deities, Stars and Doors of that particular sector to arrive at a
specific conclusion or to create a desired outcome with Qi.
Qi Men Dun Jia defines whether a sector (or Palace in the Luo Shu
grid, as it is called) is auspicious or inauspicious by such factors
such as the Stars, Doors, and Deities in that particular sector. The
5 Elements’ relationship such as Reinforce (Growth) and Restraint
(Control) interaction between the 9 Luo Shu Houses, Month Breaker
(Yue Po月破), Emptiness (空亡 Kong Wang) all play a role in determining
the severity of the Stems, Deities, Stars and Doors’ influence in that
particular sector.
Bear in mind that the Stems combos only act as a reference for
ascertaining the outlook of the situation/prediction, and not as a final
judgement of that sector.
In every Qi Men chart, there are two layers of Heavenly Stems. The first
layer is the HEAVEN Plate’s Heavenly Stem and the second layer is the
EARTH Plate’s Heavenly Stems. Combinations are then made between
the combos of the Heaven and Earth Plate.

1 	Fu Yin 伏吟
Among the 9 Stems of Three Nobles and Six Crescents, only the Three
Nobles (Yi 乙, Bing 丙, Ding 丁) and Wu 戊 are auspicious. Therefore when
the structure of Fu Yin falls on Three Nobles and Wu 戊, it basically indicates
stationary or slow progression during a positive development.
When Fu Yin falls on other Stems, it indicates stagnant status during a negative
development. However, note that the judgement should be made with
consideration with the combos of the Star, Door and Deity in that particular
House - as well as the 5 Element’s Reinforce-Restraint interactions with the
other Houses.

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 161

D
2 	Harmony 相合
When Stems in the Heaven and Earth Plates are in Harmony, affection and
connection grows. People represented by these Stems on the Heaven Plate
can usually get support from social networking, society, comrades, each
other and even attention from the opposite sex. But when Harmony falls on
negative components such as the Death Door, the situations will be completely
different.
Qi Men judges mainly by Star, Door and Deity, among which Eight Doors
are attached special priority importance. Everything has two sides; when it
comes to disease, lawsuit, and connections, a Harmony condition becomes
inauspicious as troubles tend to linger around for a long time.
3 	Opposition 相沖
The Six Crescents represents the hiding location of the six Jias and Opposition
is evident when the location of these Crests are found on Earthly Branches
that Crash with the Jia group leader. For instance:
Jia Zi’s Wu 甲子戊 meets with Jia Wu’s Xin 甲午辛. This is known as Earth
branch Zi-Wu Clash (地支子午沖).
Jia Chen’s Ren 甲辰壬 meets with Jia Xu’s Ji 甲戌己. This is known as Earth
branch Chen-Xu Clash (地支辰戌沖)
Jia Yin’s Gui 甲寅癸 meets with Jia Shen’s Geng 甲申庚. This is known as
Earth branch Yin-Shen Clash (地支寅申沖)
Opposition brings turmoil, nervousness, and disconnection among people.

4 	Earth Plate Meets Star of the Opposite Sex
Qi Men regards Yi 乙 and Geng 庚 as the rightful relationship, with Geng 庚
representing the male and Yi 乙 as the female. Geng’s lover is Ding 丁 while
Yi’s lover is Bing 丙. Moreover, Ding’s 丁 husband/boyfriend is Ren 壬 while
Bing’s 丙 wife/ girlfriend is Xin 辛.
The following combos represent potential ‘Peach Blossom’ Luck (meaning
luck with the opposite sex). However, this is just an early signal; you need to
see the interactions between the Houses of the two, for instance, Reinforce
and Restraint or Emptiness and Month Breaker, to tell if Peach Blossom Luck
has already occurred or when it is going to happen.
Stem Combo 	Explanation
Geng 庚 - Yi 乙 Man is attracted to, or cares about his
wife or partner
Yi 乙 - Geng 庚 Woman is attracted to, or cares about her
husband or partner
Geng 庚 - Ding 丁 Man is attracted to (Peach Blossom
Luck), or cares about his mistress
Yi 乙 - Bing 丙 Woman is attracted to (Peach Blossom
Luck), or cares about her lover
5 	Earth Plate meets Wu 戊 (a.k.a. Jia Zi甲子)
Wu 戊 represents capital or funds. It represents economic strength and
financial management capabilities
6 	Earth Plate meets Ji 己 (a.k.a. Jia Xu甲戌)
Xu 戌 represents Grave. Any Heavenly Stem meeting Ji 己 at the Earth Plate
represents an implies meeting the grave, which is a sign of stagnation or
slowdown. Of course, this is in general. In other cases it can also mean hidden
potential or unseen opportunities which requires ‘digging’.

7
Heavenly Stems on Heaven Plate fall into the
Graveyard of The Twelve Earthly Branches
(Year 年, Month 月, Day 日, Hour 時)
For example, when Ji 己 or Ding 丁 Stem on Heaven Plate falls at the Gen
Palace 艮宮 (Chou 丑, Gen 艮, Yin 寅). This is known as Ji 己 or Ding 丁
arriving at the Chou 丑 Grave 墓. If Chou is a Branch of that year/month/day,
it is known as Entering Graveyard in Qi Men Dun Jia.
Entering Grave or entering ‘graveyard’ has an inclination towards loss
of direction, stagnation, reduction in quantity, weakening in quality and
slowing down.
Not all structures are useful in the modern day context. Some particular
structures such as the Five Disharmony Hour 五不遇時, War Structure
戰格, Dragon Dun 龍遁 and Jade Maiden Watching The Door 玉女守門
are irrelevant and are used mainly for war craft, Feng Shui, and Date
Selection purposes. These structures have only minor influence on Qi
Map analysis and Forecasting.

164 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium

D
The 12 Birth and Growth Palaces 十二長生
The Twelve Birth and Growth Palaces are Chang Sheng 長生, Mu Yu
沐浴, Guan Dai 冠帶, Lin Guan 臨官, Di Wang 帝旺, Shuai 衰, Bing 病,
Si 死, Mu 墓, Jue 絕, Tai 胎, and Yang 養.
Just as the names suggest, “Chang Sheng (Growth)” indicates newborn
or birth; “Mu Yu (Bath)” is like the bathing and cleaning of a newborn,
showing sign of life but fragile at the same time; “Guan Dai (Youth)”
represents coming of age; “Lin Guan (Thriving)” indicates becoming an
official or pursuit of career; “Di Wang (Prosperous)” is like climbing up
to the summit, assuming important position, and even becoming a king
or emperor. According to traditional Chinese philosophy, everything
develops parabolically and so after the summit it descends to “Shuai”
(Weakening), followed by “Bing” (Sick), “Si” (Death), “Mu” (Grave), “Jue”
(Extinction), “Tai” (Conceived), and finally “Yang” (Nourishing).
After Nourishing 養, the cycle returns to Growth長生 again. From
“growing in the womb” (Yang 養 Nourishing) to “newborn” (Chang Sheng
長生Growth) the Twelve Palaces of the Five Elements are just like a life
cycle, that moves in a circular pattern.
Interestingly, Bath 沐浴 can also indicate “Bai 敗” (Failure) and is usually
associated with the star Xian Chi 咸池 (Salty Pool Star ), which is a
negative form of Peach Blossom luck, and often represents possible
adultery or excessive lust.
Some schools of metaphysics regard Growth, Prosperous, Grave and
Conceived as the “Four Valuables 四貴”, while Bath, Sick, Death and
Extinction as “ The Four Taboos 四忌”. The effects of the Twelve Palaces’
influence on the Stems, Stars, Deities and Doors need to be considered
in a Qi Men reading.

The above table illustrates the circle of life for the Five Elements of the
Ten Heavenly Stems. For example, Water element of Ren 壬 and Gui 癸
will meet with Extinction 絕 at the Death 死 branch, while Metal element
of Geng 庚 and Xin 辛 will meet with Growth 長生 at Death. Likewise,
Metal element of Geng 庚 and Xin 辛 are in the Extinction 絕 position at
Yin 寅, while Earth element of Wu 戊 and Ji 己are in the “Growth” position
at Yin 寅.
Fire of Bing 丙and Ding丁and Wood of Jia 甲 and Yi 乙 are both born at
Hai 亥. The Extinction for certain Element is at exactly where the other
Element that Produces it is born. The table does indicates that Earth
of Wu 戊 and Ji 己 are at an Extinction position at Hai 亥. However, the
same can’t be said about Fire - the Element that Produces Earth – as
the table itself doesn’t show that it is at Hai 亥 the Fire Element is born.
Not many metaphysical schools have explained this in greater detail.
Probably because Earth is in the center and Fire Produces Earth, so the
life cycle of Earth follows exactly to that of Fire.
The Grave branch for Ren 壬Water and Xin 辛 Metal is at Chen 辰; Xu 戌
is the Grave for Bing 丙 Fire, Wu 戊 Earth, and Yi 乙 Wood; Chou 丑 is
the Grave for Geng 庚 Metal, Ding 丁 Fire and Ji 己 Earth; Wei 未 is the
Grave for Jia 甲 Wood and Gui 癸 Water.
The Four Growth Palaces for the Five Elements are named the “Four
Meng 四孟” (Yin 寅, Si 巳, Shen 申Hai 亥) the Four Bath Palaces are
called “Four Zhong 四仲” (Zi 子, Wu 午, Mao 卯, You 酉), and the Four
Graves Palaces are named “Four Ji 四季” (Chen 辰, Xu 戌, Chou 丑, Wei
未). “Meng 孟”, “Zhong 仲”, and “Ji 季” here represent the first, second,
and third month respectively in a season.
Some metaphysics schools and especially the Zi Ping 子平 BaZi school
explains that as there is a Yin and Yang polarity difference among the
Heavenly Stems, and thus the cycles of the Twelve Palaces should also
be set differently and in accordance to this dissimilarity.
In general, the life of Yang 陽 energy is brought about by the death of
Yin 陰 energy, and the death of Yang energy is caused by the life or
birth of Yin energy.

甲己子午九 乙庚丑未八

Some may use Tai Xuan numbers to explain or interpret the concept of
the 60 Melodic Pillars (Liu Shi Jia Zi Na Yin 六十甲子納音).
For example, we can assign Jia 甲 and Zi 子 with the number Nine, and
Yi 乙and Chou 丑are Eight from the first line of “Jia Zi, Yi Chou, Metal in
the sea”, and this line can be summed up with the equation of 9 + 9 + 8
+ 8 = 34. 34 is then divided by Five with a balance remaining of Four, and
in the He Tu model, it says that; “Earthly Four Produces Metal 地四生金
“, which tells us that Jia Zi 甲子 and Yi Chou 乙丑 belong to the Metal
element in the Na Yin song. Further references to the 60 Na Yin can be
found in the ancient text of San Ming Tong Hui 三命通會”.
In the Chinese calendar, the Stem-Branch combos are used to indicate
the Month and Hour. How to tell what is the Stem of a month in a given
year or of an hour in a day? Let’s take a look at the so-called “Five Tigers
Chasing Year” and “Five Rats Chasing Day” methods below.

-- 185 of 810 --

174 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇

D
In the Chinese solar calendar, the first month is is known as the Tiger
(Yin 寅) month, and the second month is known as Rabbit (Mao 卯)
month and so on. These are fixed Earthly Branches. However, the
Stems for these branches are different every year. In order to determine
the Heavenly Stem for a specific month, the ancient Chinese scholars
composed a song called "Five Tigers Dun Year" as below:
甲己之年丙作首 乙庚之歲戊為頭
丙辛之歲從庚上 丁壬壬字順行流
更有戊癸何方發 甲寅之上好追求
In Jia 甲 and Ji 己 years, Bing 丙 is the head;
For Yi 乙 and Geng 庚 years, Wu 戊 comes first;
Bing 丙 and Xin 辛 years start with Geng 庚
Ding 丁and Ren 壬 years flow from Ren 壬
Wu 戊 and Gui 癸 years are led by Jia 甲
The Stem combos in the “Five Tigers” are related to the years or
months or day of the Five types of Tigers. They also represent the Five
Elements: Jia 甲 and Ji 己 combine to form Earth, Yi 乙 and Geng 庚
combine to form Metal, Bing 丙 and Xin 辛 combine to form Water,
Ding 丁and Ren 壬 combine to form Wood while Wu 戊 and Gui 癸
combine to form Fire.
Based on these Five Element interactions, Bing 丙 belongs to Fire and
Fire Produces Earth, and as Jia 甲 and Ji 己 combine to form Earth in the
years of Jia 甲 or Ji 己. Bing 丙 matches with the Yin 寅 month. This is
why Bing Yin becomes the name of the first month in that year.

-- 186 of 810 --

In the same way, Jia 甲 belongs to Wood and Wood Produces Fire. And
because Wu 戊 and Gui 癸 combine to form Fire, in the year of Wu 戊 or
Gui 癸, Jia 甲 matches with the Yin 寅 month and thus the first month of
those years are named “Jia Yin 甲寅”.

We can see from Table above that the order of the 60 Stem-Branch
combos is still the same, only with Bing Yin 丙寅 as the beginning instead
of Jia Zi. The ancient scholars emphasized heavily on the concept of
“Heaven, Earth, and Man”. So with this in mind, they regard the Zi 子
(Rat Month) as “Heaven” (The Winter Solstice in Zi 子 month is called
Heavenly Zheng 天正 Winter Solstice), while Chou 丑 month as Earth 地,
and Yin 寅 as Man 人.
According to the sequence of the 60 Pillars, Jia Zi 甲子 is followed by
Yi Chou 乙丑, which is then trailed by Bing Yin 丙寅. This explains why
a Jia 甲 year typically begins with a Bing Yin 丙寅 month. But after five
years, or 60 months, the Ji 己 year would begin with Bing Yin 丙寅 once
again. Hence, the ancient song that goes, “In Jia and Ji years, Bing is the
head” (甲己之年丙作首).
The other lines of the ancient song can be decoded similarly. This
strengthens the idea that the Five Tigers Chasing Month/Hour is just
another simple application of the “Heaven, Earth, Man” theory on
the”Stem-Branch Calendar”.
It depends largely on the”Yue Ling 月令”, or the timeliness of the Month
Branch to determine whether or not the Combination of the Heavenly
Stems can be “Transformed”.
A clearer explanation is afforded in the ancient BaZi book, Yuan Hai
Zi Ping 淵海子平, Volume III On Transformation, where it is said that
“the Combination and Transformation among Heavenly Stems represent
wisdom, and the Combination among Earthly Branches represents
blessings.” The Transformation of the life cycle of the month happens
at the Palaces of Nourishing, Grave and Thriving, while the Combination
between Yin and Yang, or Husband and Wife, is Transformed at a stage
where there is a “balance”.

As a general rule, whenever or whatever is beyond the equilibrium,
cannot Transform.
From another angle, the occurrence of a Transformation is actually
determined by the Three Harmonies and the Twelve Birth and Growth
Phases of the Month. To ensure Ding 丁 and Ren 壬 can Transform into
Wood, for instance, the Month must falls on Yin 寅, Mao 卯, Wei 未, or
Hai 亥 (Mao 卯 is Prosperous 帝旺 or peak of the Hai 亥- Mao 卯- Wei 未
Three Harmonies Wood Structure while Wei 未 is the Mu or Grave 墓 of
the Three Harmonies Wood Structure).
Take a different example of Si 巳 (Snake) month. Si 巳 is both Yang
Fire and Growth of Si 巳 - You 酉 - Chou 丑: Three Harmonies Metal
Structure. Due to this, Wu 午 and Gui 癸 can Transform into Fire, and Yi
乙and Geng 庚 can Transform into Metal.

Five Rats Dun Day 五鼠遁日
Similar to the month, the Earthly Branches of an hour is also fixed, starting
from Zi 子 hour and following the sequence of the Twelve Branches:
Chou 丑, Yin 寅, Mao 卯, Chen 辰, Si 巳 hour, Wu 午 hour as noon, then
Wei 未, Shen 申, You 酉, Xu 戌, Hai 亥, and back to Zi 子 hour again.
An hour in ancient times is equivalent to two hours in our modern clock,
and it is due to this deviation that the Zi 子 hour starts at what we now
know as 11:00pm. The ancient Chinese composed a song called the
“Five Rats” (As Zi’s 子 is represented by the Rat Animal Sign) to memorize
the hours of a day.
甲己還加甲 乙庚丙作初
丙辛從戊起 丁壬庚子居
戊癸何方發 壬子是真途
Jia 甲, Ji 己 day starts with Jia 甲;
Yi乙 , Geng 庚 day begins from Bing丙;
Bing丙 Xin辛 day commences from Wu戊;
Ding丁, Ren壬 day kicks off with Geng 庚;
Wu戊, Gui 癸 day sets off from where?
Ren Zi 壬子is the starting point.

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 181

D
According to the above 60 Pillars arrangement, a Jia 甲 day certainly
starts with Jia Zi 甲子 hour. The cycle would persist until after five days,
or sixty hours, when Ji 己 Day takes precedence and the starting hour
would return to “Jia Zi” 甲子 once again. All the other hourly combos
under the remaining Day Stem pairs follow this similar sequence of
Heavenly Stems. If you study both the tables of Five Tigers Dun and Five
Rats Dun, you would notice that both the Duns are just an application
of the Five Zis: Jia Zi 甲子, Bing Zi 丙子, Wu Zi 戊子, Geng Zi 庚子, and
Ren Zi 壬子.
It is important to discern between the Early Rat 早子 and the Late Rat
夜子 hour. In the ancient classic Si Ku Quan Shu 四庫全書 (The Imperial
Four Divisions Collection), three books have mentioned Early Rat早子
and Late Rat 夜子 hours:
1) Yuan Ben Ge Xiang Xin Shu 原本革象新書 written by Zhao You in
趙友 from the Yuan Dynasty and edited by Wang Wei 王褘 from
Ming Dynasty,
2) Tu Shu Bian 圖書編 by Zhang Huang 章潢 from Ming Dynasty,
3) San Ming Tong Hui 三命通會, which was said to be penned by Wan
Ming Ying 萬民英 from Ming Dynasty.
Yuan Ben Ge Xiang Xin Shu 原本革象新書 is a book on astronomical
calculation and assessments. Tu Shu Bian 圖書編 discusses the studies
of Yi Jing 易經, or the Book of Changes while San Ming Tong Hui 三命
通會 is about the Four Pillars of Destiny. All three were authored in Ming
Dynasty and all three expounded the concepts of the Early Rat 早子 and
Late Rat 夜子 hours.
Early and Late Rate takes the midnight as the beginning of the next day
even if it’s still within the Zi 子 Hour. Only when it’s passed midnight can
a day be regarded as a new one; any hour before that would consider
being under the same day. Here midnight is known as “Zi Zheng 子正”.
Nevertheless, such theory had not made an appearance in other works
from Ming Dynasty, nor had it appear in the publication of earlier times.
It is still devoid of proof, as of where it is originated from.

-- 193 of 810 --

182 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
D
For Qi Men chart calculation, the concept of Early and Late Rat
hours should be included to ensure the accuracy of your results.
The calculation of the traditional Chinese calendar is done according
to the waxing and waning of Yin and Yang in Yi Jing 易經, the Book of
Changes. As Zi 子 is the first of the Twelve Earthly Branches, it is also
the time when Yin wanes and Yang waxes. Likewise, the Month Branch
can be perceived in the same way as the hour.
There are 24 Jie Qi 節氣 (Solar terms ancient Chinese developed to
mark the days in 24 divisions of a year) in a year, and in average every
month Branch has two Jie Qi 節氣; the first one being Jie 節 (or Jie Qi 節
氣) and the second one called Qi 氣 (or Zhong Qi 中氣).
Chu 初 and Zheng 正 can also be used to represent the first half
and the second half of the month. For instance, the first Jie Qi 節氣
of the Zi 子 month (Yang waxes gradually) is Heavy Snow, so it is
called Zi Chu 子初 (Heavy Snow); the second Jie Qi 節氣 is Winter
Solstice, wherefore it is called Tian Zheng 天正 Winter Solstice.
While differences are noted in Chu 初 and Zheng 正, it is important
to know that the Branch month should start from Jie 節 of the month,
instead of Qi 氣. Therefore a new day should also be regarded as
starting from Zi Chu 子初 (Conversion from night to day).

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 183

D
Waxing and Waning of Yin and Yang and the Auspiciousness-
Inauspiciousness of the Heavenly Stems
In Qi Men:
甲、乙、丙、丁、戊 前五位為五陽時，
己、庚、辛、壬、癸 後五位為五陰時。
Jia 甲, Yi 乙, Bing 丙, Ding 丁, Wu 戊 are the five Yang hours;
Ji 己, Geng 庚, Xin 辛, Ren 壬, Gui 癸 are the five Yin hours.
In Yang hours, the advantage is with the Guest instead of the Host, so
it is advisable to take the initiative or be proactive. In general, the Five
Yang hours contains more auspicious combos, therefore they are good
times to go for military expedition, travel afar for business endeavors,
and making new developments or simple for building a house. On the
contrary, escaping and hiding away are not to be conducted.
“古人云:得陽幹者飛而不止，利客先舉。” 	To 	quote 	from 	an 	ancient
saying: One with Yang Stems will rise without stopping; for the initiative,
it is the time to strive.
When it is a Yin hour, the advantage is with the host instead of the guest,
so it is advisable to wait and anticipate your opponent’s first move.
The Five Yin hours usually contain more inauspicious configurations.
Therefore, when facing enemy’s army or opponents’ imminent attack,
waiting till the enemy make the first move can bring victory. It is not
a good time for assuming official positions, moving, traveling, getting
married, starting a new business or making a deal. It is, however, a good
time to remain passive, prepare, plan secretly, bide for the right time and
wait for the right opportunity to strike. Also good for escape.
“古人云:得陰乾利為主，伏而不起則利。” To quote from an ancient
saying: One with Yin hours have advantage to themselves; wait it out
until things turn out well for you.

Gui 癸 Water
Gui 癸 Water, being a Water element, is always on the go, albeit in a
slower pace compared to its Yang water counterpart. With a high sense
of principles, they are often perceived as patient, tolerant, creative and
passive individuals. Key qualities: Imaginative, creative, good teachers,
introverts
A. The 10 Stem Attributes
奇門代表
Name in Qi Men
Gui 癸 is known as the Heavenly Conceal
天藏, a star good for demonstrating power
and executing a large plan, and saving or
harvesting. In some literature, it is also known
as the Heavenly Net or Elegant Seal 華蓋.
代表
Represent
Things or people that represent female
figure. Also denotes ideas, thoughts and
philosophies.
五行
Five Element Yin Water 陰水
方向
Direction North 北
季節

月份

The 8 Doors 八門
Introduction
The Door Pan 門盤 or Door Plate in Qi Men Dun Jia 奇門遁甲 is also
known as the Man Plate 人盤. This Plate is usually used to analyze the
outcome of man’s action and activities.
Among the 8 Doors in Qi Men, the Doors of Open 開, Rest 休 and Life 生
are regarded as Auspicious Doors. These Doors correspond to the Luo
Shu 洛書 Palaces of Qian 乾 6 (NW), Kan 坎 1 (N) and Gen 艮 8 (NE).

Center
5
The Open Door (開門), as the name implies, represents the opening
of an establishment, or to open up the opening up of the channels of
Qi Flow. It represents livelihood and progression. This Door therefore
represents a person’s lifelong career, business or official position.
The Rest Door (休門) represents calm, relaxation and rejuvenation. It
belongs to the element of Water. This Door usually governs home-based
or domestic affairs like marriage, family relationship and health.
The Life Door (生門), on the other hand, represents the source of life.
It governs one’s wealth, which includes assets, properties, house and
material wealth. It represents one’s lifelong financial fortune.

-- 308 of 810 --

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 297
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
E
Career, wealth, marriage and family are the biggest concerns in life for
anyone. Therefore, these three Doors are perhaps the most important
analysis points in a Qi Men Dun Jia reading if we want to check one’s
degree of happiness and success in career or business.
The Doors of Death 死 , Fear 驚 and Harm 傷 are inauspicious. The three
issues governed by these three Doors are illness (possibly leading to
death), fear and anxiety, and pain. These issues may come from career
related problems, financial challenges and/or lawsuits. While at times
they may also rise from physical injury (from unexpected disaster) and/
or mental stress arising from personal, work or social life. Whenever we
want to analyze these factors, we would study the Doors of Death, Fear
and Harm in the Qi Men Dun Jia chart.
Delusion 杜 and Scenery 景 Doors are considered neutral Doors. They
govern the private or public lives of the person in question. If there are
good combinations like positive Stem Combos, positive Stars, Deities
and/or they are in the position of timeliness like Prosperous and Strong,
the Scenery and Delusion Doors can bring about positive outcomes.
Conversely, if there were bad structures, Stars, Deities and/or if they are
at the untimely positions like the Weak and Trap state, these two Doors
would be considered inauspicious.
Take note that the Doors’ classifications as auspicious or inauspicious
are generic. In actual practice, the practitioner needs to judge the
outcome by taking into consideration the condition of the Stars, Deities
and Structure in relation to the topic of discourse. Different conditions,
even with the same set up, may wield a different outcome.
For instance, we know that the Open Door is an auspicious Door. But
when we are analyzing or forecasting one’s health, especially in relation
to pregnancy, the Open Door may not always be a good sign. This is
especially true if and when the Open Door is in the same Palace as the
White Tiger, as this denotes the need for emergency surgery. In other
cases when the forecast’s Useful God lands on an Open Door, it means
that the issues have been exposed and that they are no longer confidential.

-- 309 of 810 --

298 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
E
Life Door, for example, is considered an auspicious Door. When
we are forecasting the outcome of a lost person, the meeting of the
Life Door is not such a good idea. This is because the Door of Life
shows that the missing person will not return home temporarily.
On the contrary, if Useful God lands on the Doors of Death, Fear
or Harm, it infers that the missing person has nowhere further to
go but to return home, or that he has a good chance to be found.
Also, even if Death Door is an inauspicious Door, but when it comes to
matters related to funeral and burial, hunting and fishing, and if the Useful
God lands on the Door of Death, it will indicate a positive outcome. On
the contrary, if the Useful God lands on the Door of Life when dealing
with the above issues, it will indicate a misfortune.
The Harm Door, for instance, is usually not beneficial when it comes
to doing business, travelling, building or repairing buildings, or getting
married. However, this Door is perfect when it comes to activities like
collecting debt, gambling, competing with others, and even activities
like catching/fishing or hunting illegally.

-- 310 of 810 --

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 299
奇

遁
甲
寶
鑑
E
The Five Elements of The 8 Doors
Each of the 8 Doors has their own set of unique qualities. The Doors
of Open, Rest and Life are the three auspicious Doors (三吉門), which
are extremely favorable for all activities and forecasting matters, if and
when they are combined with the three Nobles to form an auspicious
structure.
However, the Doors must also be in the position of Prosperous and
Strong at the correct timing of the year (depending on the element of
the Season), and not be negatively influenced by other factors such as
being Restricted and Compelled by the Palace it resides in.
There are two principles to decide whether the 8 Doors are Prosperous
or Strong:
1. The Five Elements’ relationship between the Doors and Palaces
2. The Season of the Year

-- 311 of 810 --

300 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium
奇
門
遁
甲

鑑
E
Understand these terms:
Door Compelling 門迫, Palace Compelling 宮迫, Harmony
和, Justice 義, Fu Yin and Fan Yin 伏吟和返吟
A. Door Compelling 門迫 is the term used to describe the situation
where the element of the Door Counters the element of the Palace in
which it resides.
When auspicious Doors are Compelled, the positive qualities cannot
be expected. Likewise, when the inauspicious Doors are Compelled,
less of the negative aspects would show.
Note: Yang Counters Yang and Yin Counters Yin. Doors and Palaces of
the same polarity tend to Counter each other. The classification of
Yin and Yang for 8 Doors depends on the Luo Shu Nine Palaces
of the Yin or Yang trigram.
陽
In Yang trigram
Qian Open 乾開
Kan Rest 坎休
Gen Life 艮生
Zhen Harm 震傷
陰
In Yin trigram
Xun Delusion 巽杜
Li Scenery 離景
Kun Death 坤死
Dui Fear 兌驚

C. Harmony 和 is the term used to describe the situation where the
element of the Door is Producing its Palace.
D. Justice 義 is the term used to describe the situation where the
element of the Palace is Producing the Door.
If the Door and its Palace were inter-producing, this situation would
be highly favorable. But if it were an inauspicious Door produced by
a Prosperous or Strong Season, the situation would bring forth much
negativity.
E. Fu Yin 伏吟
After arrangement of the Envoy following Palaces of the Hour, the
8 Doors on the Man Plate and their original Palaces are in the same
position as seen in the diagram below. This situation is called Fu Yin.
For example, if the Rest Door is in Kan Palace, Life Door will be in
Gen Palace.

F. Fan Yin 返吟
Positions of the 8 Doors on the Man Plate and their original Palaces
are at the opposite Palace. For example, the Life Door is in the Kun
Palace, which is opposite to its original Gen Palace.

8 Doors Fan Yin - When the 8 Doors are in the Palaces opposite to their
original natal Palaces, everything is inauspicious and unusable even
though there are Noble configurations.

this, Life in Life and Death in Death. Provided inauspicious Palaces have

生兮死在死，假令凶宿得奇門，萬事皆凶不堪使.”

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 305
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
E
The idea to determine the 8 Doors’ Five Elements has something in
common with the 8 Solar Terms of the Eight Trigrams. This is because
the 8 Doors are derived from the Eight Trigrams Extent Numbers - the
“inferring states of 8 Solar Terms of the 8 Doors.”
Example:
• Kan坎 Trigram from the Winter Solstice Controls has three solar terms:
the Winter Solstice, Lesser Cold and Greater Cold. This cold seasons
is where the Kan Trigram and the Rest Door are Prosperous 旺.
• Spring follows soon after with another three solar terms. It starts with
the Beginning of Spring, where the Gen 艮 Trigram and the Life Door
would be in Strong spirits.
• The three solar terms that follow after the Spring Equinox belong to
Zhen 震. Here, the Harm Door is brewing in a manner similar to a fetal
respiration, and thus it is called Conceived 胎.
• The three solar terms after the Beginning of Summer belong to Xun巽
of the Delusion Door. As the spirit of Summer is exhausted in Winter,
it is named Absent沒.
• The three solar terms after the Summer Solstice are in Li 離 of the
Scenery Door. As Fire is hard to Produce in the cold winter, and it is
also Countered to Death by Kan 坎 Water, it is called Death 死.
• This is followed by the next three solar terms after Beginning of Autumn,
which are in the Kun 坤 sector of the Death Door. As Weakening Earth
Counters when Water is Prosperous, it is called Trap囚.
• Three solar terms after the Autumn Equinox are in Dui 兌 of the Fear
Door. As it is a time of Rest when Weakening Metal Produces and
Water is Prosperous, it is called Rest 休.
• Three solar terms after Beginning of Winter are in Qian 乾 of the
Open Door. As Weakening Metal Produces Prosperous Water, it is
Abandoned 廢.

The 8 Doors are an extremely important factor in Qi Men Dun Jia. It is
crucial to study its contents closely in every Qi Men reading. Doors of
Open, Rest and Life are the three auspicious Doors. If they are positioned
together with the three Nobles 三奇 - Yi 乙Noble, Bing 丙 Noble and
Ding丁 Noble - they can exert greater positive energy.
There are also important mentions about the 8 Doors’ function in The
Fisherman’ Song 煙波釣叟歌, where it goes like this: “Everything meeting
with Doors of Open, Rest and Life will be good in the end. When in Harm,
it is good for hunting and there will be a big harvest. Delusion is suitable
for invitation and hiding, and thus being invisible. In Scenery, one can
make an anonymous appeal and escape from prison. In Fear, one can
win litigation and obtain a reputation. Regarding Death, it is only suitable
for hanging or execution.” 八門若遇開休生，諸事逢之總稱情，傷宜捕獵
終須獲，杜好邀遮及隱形，景上投書并破陣，驚能擒訟有聲名，若值死
門何所主，只宜吊死與行刑。

308 Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium

寶
鑑
E
Note on ‘Evidence’ Occurrence in Qi Men Dun Jia
In this system of metaphysics, especially under the school of Fa Qi Men
法奇門, the observation of ‘evidence’ occurring after specific Qi Men
Dun Jia hour selected is very important. When the ‘evidence’ occurs,
this denotes that the prediction or the expected outcome will become a
reality. It is similar to receiving a response from the universe on whether
you have selected the hour correctly.
The following section is almost a verbatim translation of the original
texts on Evidence Occurrence (奇門剋應). You need to exercise
some genuine creativity, logic and maturity in interpreting what these
‘evidence’ would look like in real life. For instance, some of the text may
say that one would see a ‘Snake Biting a Pig’ in a certain direction. If
you live in the city, it is extremely unlikely you will bump into a pig on
the street that would be bitten by a snake!
A snake mentioned in the old text could relate to things or objects that
look like a snake. For instance, cables, long pipes, ropes, and long
and winding roads. A pig may be referring to someone born in the year
of the Pig or the Northwest direction (since the Pig resides in the NW
direction). And when the text talks about a horse carriage, in today’s
world, this would be a car. The text may specifically mention seeing a
‘horse’ and a carriage, so this could mean a car with a horse logo. A
Mustang or a Ferrari might fit this description today.
Another common term used in Qi Men Dun Jia texts is the term ‘Nobleman
or Gui Ren 貴人'. When the text talks about meeting a Nobleman, it means
meeting someone who has stature, a respectable title, high social status,
a mentor, a teacher or a widely-esteemed person or someone who is
basically helpful or can be helpful to you. For the sake of easy translation,
I use the term “Nobleman” without further explanation or description as
to what specific person this would be. You will need to exercise your
judgment during the reading process to determine who this person is or
how he or she would manifest in real life.
As you can see, the list of possibilities goes on. It will be important for
you to exercise your own thinking and judgment when interpreting this
section. If you’re still unsure, it is best to check with your Qi Men Dun
Jia teacher or someone with enough experience with this particular Qi
Men technique.

E
The Open Door 開門
Introduction
The Open Door is a Door for establishing and advocating a business
or business related activities. Qian represents Heaven, father, extreme
Yang, head, emperor and leader. Qian’s Najia 納甲 is Ren 壬 and Jia 甲.
The Qian Palace contains the branch Hai 亥 (Pig). Inside Hai hides Ren
Water and Jia Wood. Based on the Twelve Birth and Growth Phases
theory, Hai is the Growth (長生 Chang Sheng) of Jia Wood. Hai is also
the Officer Position (臨官 Lin Guan) of Ren Water. As Jia is the head of
the Ten Heavenly Stems and Water is the leader of the Five Elements,
this is why we call this the Open Door.
The Open Door’s original home Palace is in the Qian 乾 Palace in the
Northwest. Its energy is Prosperous in Autumn, especially on days of
Xu 戌 and Hai 亥; Strong in the last months of every Season, Weak in
Winter, Trapped in Spring and Dead in Summer.
When the Open Door resides in its home Palace of Qian, it is called
“Fu Yin 伏吟". If it resides in the Xun Palace 4, it is called “Fan Yin 反
吟". When the Open Door is in the Gen 艮 Palace 8, it is called Entering
Graveyard 入墓, and if it is in the Li 離 Palace 9, it becomes Countered
受制. In the Kun 坤 Palace 2, it is most Prosperous 大吉 and when it is in
the Dui 兌 Palace 7, it is Prosperous and Strong 旺相. Open Door in the
Kan 坎 Palace 1 is moderately auspicious 次吉, and while in the Zhen
震 Palace 3, it is Compelled 被迫.

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 327
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
休
Rest
E
The Rest Door 休門
Introduction
The Rest Door is the Door of rehabilitation. Kan represents Water, young
man, Winter, reversion, and the arrival of Spring. This Door’s timing
refers to the moment where the Universe returns to Rest and begins to
grow once again; this is why it is called the Rest Door.
When Rest Door resides in its home Palace of Kan, it is called “Fu Yin 伏
吟". If it resides in Li Palace, it is called “Fan Yin 反吟". When Rest Door
is in the Xun Palace, it is called Entering Graveyard 入墓, and if it is in the
Kun and Gen Palaces, it becomes Countered 受制. In the Qian Palace, it
is Greater Luck, and when it is in the Dui Palace, it is Lesser Luck.
Water of Rest is the most Yin and actually belongs to Aquarius. It
denotes that all things on the Earth take Water as a matter of life and
death. The so-called Zi is the beginning of the coming of Spring, when
plants begin to bud. Rest is the Door of returning to the origin and the
source, and therefore, it is auspicious. If Rest is harmonious with Ding
Noble and close to Great Moon, it is Man Dun and with it, everything is
auspicious. Rest is Prosperous in Zhen, Strong in Kan, and is the life in
Qian and Dui Palaces. These situations are all auspicious. Rest in Kun,
Gen and Center Palaces is Countered, with Entering Graveyard in Xun
Palace and Fan Yin in Li. All these situations are inauspicious.

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 345
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
生
Life
E
The Life Door 生門
Introduction
Life Door is the Door of grandeur. Gen represents mountain and
boys. After the Spring Equinox, everything revives again, and the
Yang spirit increases gradually. As Earth generates everything, it is
called the Life Door.
When Life Door resides in its home Palace of Gen (8) in the Northeast,
it is called “Fu Yin 伏吟". If it resides in Kun Palace 2, it is called “Fan
Yin 反吟". When Rest Door is in the Xun Palace 4, it is called Entering
Graveyard 入墓, and if it is in the Zhen Palace 3, it becomes Restricted.
In the Qian Palace 6, it is Greater Auspicious, and when it is in the
Dui Palace 7, it is Discouraged. It is Forced when Life Door is in Kan
Palace.
Life Door is in Gen Palace, and Gen is at Yin. The Heaven opens at Zi,
the Earth breaks at Chou, and Man is born at Yin. At the same moment,
three Yang are available for bliss, and thus all things on Earth will grow.
A new lease of life grows and spreads to all things on the Earth. Thus,
it is called the most auspicious Door. When Life Door is in Qian and Dui
Palaces, it is Prosperous. When Life Door is in Palaces of Kun, Gen and
Center, it is Strong. When it is in Kan Palace, it is Compelled. In the Zhen
Palace, Life Door is Countered by Wood. In the Xun Palace, it is Entering
Graveyard, and this is inauspicious. When born in the Li Palace, it is
auspicious.

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 361
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
傷
Harm
E
The Harm Door 傷門
Introduction
The Harm Door is a Door of the six evils in the Zhen Palace, which is
located in the East, and belongs to the Wood element. After the Spring
Equinox, Jia Wood is Prosperous. However, if it is overly exposed to
prosperity, Jia Wood is likely to “break”.
Zhen represents the eldest son, thunder and movement, but a movement
that leads to hurt. If Jia Zi Wu is in Zhen Palace, it means Zi and Mao
punish and hurt each other. Therefore, it is called a Harm Door.
When Harm Door resides in its home Palace of Zhen (3) in the East,
it is called “Fu Yin 伏吟". If it resides in Dui Palace 7, it is called “Fan
Yin 反吟". When Harm Door is in the Kun Palace 2, it is called Entering
Graveyard 入墓, and if it is in the Kan Palace 1, it becomes Greater
Auspicious. In the Qian Palace 6, it is Restricted, and when it is in the
Gen Palace 8, it is Compelled and Greater Inauspicious. In the Li Palace
9 until the Xun Palace 4, it is Discouraged. 居巽四宮為此

The Wood of Harm Door is in the Spring Equinox. As Wood’s fluid flows
from the inside and evaporates on the outside, it causes too much
leakage, which explains an inward emptiness, or lacking of labor and
toil. By the second month, newly-grown layer of armour or bark is least
resistant to the coldness of dew. Therefore, the Harm Door is considered
inauspicious. If the Noble meets with the Harm Door, it is only suitable
for catching runaway thieves, fishing and hunting, collecting debts and
gambling. If a person assumes a new job, starts off a journey, gets
married, handles business, or builds or repairs buildings, these would
be unsuitable and inauspicious.

-- 388 of 810 --

Qi Men Dun Jia Compendium 377
奇
門
遁
甲
寶
鑑
杜
Delusion
E
The Delusion Door 杜門
Introduction
The Delusion Door is a Door that originates from the Xun Palace of the
Southeast. It belongs to the Wood element.
Xun represents the Southeast, Wood and wind. As Heaven resides over
at the Northwest, the Earth would take sit in the Southeast. As such,
the Southeast represents the land of obstacles and congestion. Xun
also represents the oldest sister, but it is countered by the father in the
Qian (Metal) Palace. This palace, being wood element, then Counters
the mother in Kun which is Earth, and denotes that the relationship
with parents is poor. Therefore, it denotes hindrances with issues and
emotions and thus, it is called the Delusion Door. A door of blockages
or hindrances.
When Delusion Door resides in its home Palace of Xun, it is called “Fu
Yin 伏吟". If it resides in Qian Palace 6, it is called “Fan Yin 反吟". When
Delusion Door is in the Dui Palace 7, it is called Entering Graveyard 入
墓, and if it is in the Dui Palace 7, it becomes Countered 受制. In the
Gen Palace 8, it is Compelled and when it is in the Kan Palace 1, it is
Prosperous. Delusion Door in the Zhen Palace 3 is Harmonious, and
while in the Li Palace 9, it is Discouraged.

Delusion Door belongs to the Wood element and it resides in the
beginning period of Summer. As all energies thrived outside and fluid
leaked, the Yang energy has reached its peak and the Yin energy will
emerge. At this moment, the Wood nature has been exhausted. It wants
to restrain itself but fails; it wants to strive up but its energy is worn
down. It wants to keep its energy to conserve its descendants, and yet
fear to hurt them if it hides them in a hard and rough place. Overall, it is
less inauspicious for Delusion Door. Delusion Door is a place of hiding
and is suitable for avoiding the incoming trouble, blocking caves and
hunting. It is unsuitable for other matters.
